     <p>
      <b>
       Thorium
      </b>
      is a
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">
       chemical element
      </a>
      with symbol
      <b>
       Th
      </b>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">
       atomic number
      </a>
      90. A
      <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">
       radioactive
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">
       actinide
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">
       metal
      </a>
      , thorium is one of only two significantly radioactive elements that still occur naturally in large quantities as a
      <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">
       primordial element
      </a>
      (the other being
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">
       uranium
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        [a]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was discovered in 1829 by the Norwegian priest and amateur mineralogist
      <a href="/wiki/Morten_Thrane_Esmark" title="Morten Thrane Esmark">
       Morten Thrane Esmark
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krebs2006_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Krebs2006-4">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and identified by the Swedish chemist
      <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">
       Jöns Jacob Berzelius
      </a>
      , who named it after
      <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">
       Thor
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Germanic_deities" title="List of Germanic deities">
       Norse god
      </a>
      of thunder.
     </p>
     <p>
      A thorium atom has 90
      <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
       protons
      </a>
      and therefore 90
      <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
       electrons
      </a>
      , of which four are
      <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">
       valence electrons
      </a>
      . Thorium metal is silvery and
      <a href="/wiki/Tarnish" title="Tarnish">
       tarnishes
      </a>
      black when exposed to air, forming the dioxide. Thorium is weakly radioactive: all its known
      <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">
       isotopes
      </a>
      are unstable. Thorium-232 (
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th), which has 142
      <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">
       neutrons
      </a>
      , is the most stable isotope of thorium and accounts for nearly all natural thorium, with six other natural isotopes occurring only as
      <a href="/wiki/Trace_radioisotope" title="Trace radioisotope">
       trace radioisotopes
      </a>
      . Thorium has the longest half-life of all the significantly radioactive elements, 14.05 billion years; it decays very slowly through
      <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">
       alpha decay
      </a>
      to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radium-228" title="Radium-228">
       radium-228
      </a>
      (
      <sup>
       228
      </sup>
      Ra), starting a
      <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">
       decay chain
      </a>
      named the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorium_series" title="Thorium series">
       thorium series
      </a>
      that ends at stable
      <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">
       lead
      </a>
      -208 (
      <sup>
       208
      </sup>
      Pb). Thorium is estimated to be about three to four times more
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">
       abundant
      </a>
      than uranium in the Earth's crust, and is chiefly refined from
      <a href="/wiki/Monazite" title="Monazite">
       monazite
      </a>
      sands as a by-product of extracting
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rare_earth_metal" title="Rare earth metal">
       rare earth metals
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Thorium was once commonly used as the light source in
      <a href="/wiki/Gas_mantle" title="Gas mantle">
       gas mantles
      </a>
      and as an alloying material, but these applications have declined due to concerns about its radioactivity. Thorium is still widely used as an alloying element in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TIG_welding" title="TIG welding">
       TIG welding
      </a>
      electrodes (at a rate of 1–2% mix with tungsten).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It remains popular as a material in high-end optics and scientific instrumentation; thorium and uranium are the only significantly radioactive elements with major commercial applications that do not rely on their radioactivity. Thorium is predicted to be able to replace uranium as nuclear fuel in
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">
       nuclear reactors
      </a>
      , but only a few
      <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle#Reactors" title="Thorium fuel cycle">
       thorium reactors
      </a>
      have yet been completed.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bulk_properties">
       Bulk properties
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Bulk properties">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Thorium is a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hardness_(materials_science)" title="Hardness (materials science)">
       soft
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetism" title="Paramagnetism">
       paramagnetic
      </a>
      , bright silvery radioactive actinide metal. In the
      <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">
       periodic table
      </a>
      , it is located to the right of the actinide
      <a href="/wiki/Actinium" title="Actinium">
       actinium
      </a>
      , to the left of the actinide
      <a href="/wiki/Protactinium" title="Protactinium">
       protactinium
      </a>
      , and below the
      <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">
       lanthanide
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Cerium" title="Cerium">
       cerium
      </a>
      . Pure thorium is very
      <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">
       ductile
      </a>
      and, as normal for metals, can be
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold-rolling#Cold_rolling" title="Cold-rolling">
       cold-rolled
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Swaging" title="Swaging">
       swaged
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Drawing_(manufacturing)" title="Drawing (manufacturing)">
       drawn
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      At room temperature, thorium metal has a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Face-centred_cubic" title="Face-centred cubic">
       face-centred cubic
      </a>
      crystal structure with lattice parameter
      <i>
       a
      </i>
      = 508.42 pm; additionally, it has two other forms at exotic conditions, one at high temperature (over 1360 °C;
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body-centred_cubic" title="Body-centred cubic">
       body-centred cubic
      </a>
      ) and one at high pressure (around 100 GPa;
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body-centred_tetragonal" title="Body-centred tetragonal">
       body-centred tetragonal
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The properties of thorium vary widely depending on the amount of impurities in the sample: the major impurity is usually
      <a href="/wiki/Thorium_dioxide" title="Thorium dioxide">
       thorium dioxide
      </a>
      (ThO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      ). The purest thorium specimens usually contain about a tenth of a percent of the dioxide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Experimental measurements of its density give values between 11.5 and 11.66 g/cm
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      : these are slightly lower than the theoretically expected value of 11.724 g/cm
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      calculated from thorium's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lattice_parameter" title="Lattice parameter">
       lattice parameters
      </a>
      , perhaps due to microscopic voids forming in the metal when it is cast.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These values lie intermediate between those of its neighbours actinium (10.07 g/cm
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      ) and protactinium (15.37 g/cm
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      ), showing the continuity of trends across the early actinides.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thorium's melting point of 1750 °C is above both that of actinium (1227 °C) and that of protactinium (approximately 1560 °C). In the beginning of period 7, from
      <a href="/wiki/Francium" title="Francium">
       francium
      </a>
      to thorium, the melting points of the elements increase (following the trend in the other periods): this is because the number of delocalised electrons that each atom contributes increases from one in francium to four in thorium, and there is a greater attraction between these electrons and the metal ions as their charge increases from one in francium to four in thorium. After thorium there is a new smooth trend downward in the melting points of the early actinides from thorium to
      <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">
       plutonium
      </a>
      where the number of f electrons increases from zero to six, due to the increasing hybridisation of the 5f and 6d orbitals and the formation of directional bonds in the metal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-7">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Among the actinides, thorium has the highest melting and boiling points and second-lowest density (second only to actinium) Its boiling point of 4788 °C is the fifth-highest among all the elements with known boiling points, behind only
      <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">
       osmium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">
       tantalum
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">
       tungsten
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">
       rhenium
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thorium has a
      <a href="/wiki/Bulk_modulus" title="Bulk modulus">
       bulk modulus
      </a>
      of 54
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigapascal" title="Gigapascal">
       GPa
      </a>
      , comparable to those of
      <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">
       tin
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">
       scandium
      </a>
      . The hardness of thorium is similar to that of soft
      <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">
       steel
      </a>
      , so heated pure thorium can be rolled in sheets and pulled into wire.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-7">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nevertheless, while thorium is nearly half as dense as
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">
       uranium
      </a>
      and plutonium, it is harder than either of them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_7-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-7">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thorium becomes
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superconductor" title="Superconductor">
       superconductive
      </a>
      below 1.4
      <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
       K
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thorium can also form
      <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">
       alloys
      </a>
      with many other metals. Addition of small amounts of thorium improves the mechanical strength of
      <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">
       magnesium
      </a>
      , and thorium-
      <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">
       aluminium
      </a>
      alloys have been considered as a way to store thorium in proposed future thorium nuclear reactors. With
      <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">
       chromium
      </a>
      and uranium, it forms
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eutectic_mixture" title="Eutectic mixture">
       eutectic mixtures
      </a>
      , and thorium is completely
      <a href="/wiki/Miscibility" title="Miscibility">
       miscible
      </a>
      in both solid and liquid
      <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">
       states
      </a>
      with its lighter
      <a href="/wiki/Congener_(chemistry)" title="Congener (chemistry)">
       congener
      </a>
      cerium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">
       Isotopes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Isotopes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_thorium" title="Isotopes of thorium">
       Isotopes of thorium
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2020" data-file-width="1310" height="463" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg/300px-Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg/450px-Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg/600px-Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Decay_Chain_Thorium.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The 4
        <i>
         n
        </i>
        <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">
         decay chain
        </a>
        of
        <sup>
         232
        </sup>
        Th, commonly called the "thorium series"
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Although every element up to
      <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">
       bismuth
      </a>
      (element 83) has an isotope that is practically stable for all purposes ("classically stable"), with the exceptions of
      <a href="/wiki/Technetium" title="Technetium">
       technetium
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">
       promethium
      </a>
      (elements 43 and 61), all elements from
      <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">
       polonium
      </a>
      (element 84) onward are noticeably radioactive. Of these, thorium (element 90) is the most stable, closely followed by uranium (element 92): the isotope
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th has a half-life of 14.05 billion years, about three times the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_earth" title="Age of the earth">
       age of the earth
      </a>
      , and even slightly longer than the generally accepted
      <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">
       age of the universe
      </a>
      (about 13.8 billion years). As such,
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th still occurs naturally today: four-fifths of the thorium present at Earth's formation has survived to the present.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-8">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thorium and uranium are thus the most well-studied of all the radioactive elements.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CottonSA2006_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CottonSA2006-11">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The heaviest three primordial nuclides,
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th,
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">
       <sup>
        235
       </sup>
       U
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">
       <sup>
        238
       </sup>
       U
      </a>
      , are the only isotopes beyond bismuth that have half-lives measured in billions of years. Thus, they are the only such heavy isotopes to have survived since their production around ten billion years ago. Hence, thorium and uranium are the only important
      <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">
       primordial
      </a>
      radioactive elements. Even then, the half-life of
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th is only a billionth that of
      <sup>
       209
      </sup>
      Bi, the last classically stable nuclide.
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th is the only isotope of thorium occurring in significant quantities in nature today, and thus thorium is usually considered to be a
      <a href="/wiki/Mononuclidic_element" title="Mononuclidic element">
       mononuclidic element
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-8">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The reason for the existence of this "island of relative stability" at thorium and uranium, where the longest-lived isotopes have half-lives of millions or billions of years, is because the most stable isotopes of these elements have closed nuclear shells;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      nevertheless, since thorium and uranium have more protons than bismuth, their nuclei are thus still susceptible to
      <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">
       alpha decay
      </a>
      because the strong nuclear force is not strong enough to overcome the electromagnetic repulsion between their protons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beiser_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-beiser-14">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th is the longest-lived isotope in the 4
      <i>
       n
      </i>
      decay chain which includes isotopes with a
      <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">
       mass number
      </a>
      divisible by 4 (hence the name: it is also called the thorium series after its progenitor). The chain begins with the alpha decay of
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th to
      <sup>
       228
      </sup>
      Ra, and terminates at stable
      <sup>
       208
      </sup>
      Pb.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_8-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-8">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      (
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th very occasionally undergoes
      <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_fission" title="Spontaneous fission">
       spontaneous fission
      </a>
      rather than alpha decay, and has left evidence in doing so in its minerals, but the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partial_half-life" title="Partial half-life">
       partial half-life
      </a>
      of this process is very large at over 10
      <sup>
       21
      </sup>
      years and hence alpha decay predominates.)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-15">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th is so long-lived, its daughters still exist in nature as
      <a href="/wiki/Radiogenic_nuclide" title="Radiogenic nuclide">
       radiogenic nuclides
      </a>
      despite their much shorter half-lives, the longest among them being the 5.7-year half-life of
      <sup>
       228
      </sup>
      Ra, the immediate alpha daughter of
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th. Any sample of thorium or its compounds contain traces of these daughters, which are isotopes of
      <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">
       thallium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">
       lead
      </a>
      , bismuth, polonium,
      <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">
       radon
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">
       radium
      </a>
      , and actinium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_8-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-8">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As such, natural thorium samples can be chemically purified to extract its useful daughter nuclides, such as
      <sup>
       212
      </sup>
      Pb, which is used in
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_medicine" title="Nuclear medicine">
       nuclear medicine
      </a>
      for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cancer_therapy" title="Cancer therapy">
       cancer therapy
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thirty
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope">
       radioisotopes
      </a>
      have been characterised, which range in mass number from 209
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ikezoe_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ikezoe-19">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      to 238.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_15-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-15">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most stable of them (after
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th) are
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th with a half-life of 75,380 years,
      <sup>
       229
      </sup>
      Th with a half-life of 7,340 years,
      <sup>
       228
      </sup>
      Th with a half-life of 1.92 years,
      <sup>
       234
      </sup>
      Th with a half-life of 24.10 days, and
      <sup>
       227
      </sup>
      Th with a half-life of 18.68 days: all of these isotopes occur in nature as
      <a href="/wiki/Trace_radioisotope" title="Trace radioisotope">
       trace radioisotopes
      </a>
      due to their presence in the decay chains of
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th,
      <sup>
       235
      </sup>
      U,
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U, and
      <sup>
       237
      </sup>
      Np (produced in minute traces in nuclear reactions in uranium ores). All of the remaining thorium isotopes have half-lives that are less than thirty days and the majority of these have half-lives that are less than ten minutes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_8-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-8">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In deep
      <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">
       seawaters
      </a>
      the isotope
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th becomes significant enough that the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">
       International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry
      </a>
      (IUPAC) reclassified thorium as a binuclidic element in 2013, as it can then make up to 0.04% of natural thorium, with
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th being the remainder.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUPAC2013_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IUPAC2013-20">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The reason for this is that while its parent
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U is soluble in water,
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th is insoluble and thus precipitates to form part of the sediment, and may be observed doing so. Uranium ores with low thorium concentrations can be purified to produce gram-sized thorium samples of which over a quarter is the
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th isotope, since
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th is one of the daughters of
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_15-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-15">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thorium thus has a characteristic terrestrial isotopic composition, and so an
      <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">
       atomic mass
      </a>
      can be given, which is 232.0377(4)
      <a href="/wiki/Unified_atomic_mass_unit" title="Unified atomic mass unit">
       u
      </a>
      . Thorium is one of only three significantly radioactive elements (the others being protactinium and uranium) that occur in large enough quantities on Earth for this to be possible.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUPAC2013_20-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IUPAC2013-20">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thorium also has three known
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">
       nuclear isomers
      </a>
      (or metastable states),
      <sup>
       216m1
      </sup>
      Th,
      <sup>
       216m2
      </sup>
      Th, and
      <sup>
       229m
      </sup>
      Th.
      <sup>
       229m
      </sup>
      Th has the lowest known
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Excitation_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Excitation energy (page does not exist)">
       excitation energy
      </a>
      of any isomer,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruchowska_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ruchowska-21">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      measured to be (7.6 ± 0.5) eV. This is so low that when it undergoes
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isomeric_transition" title="Isomeric transition">
       isomeric transition
      </a>
      , the emitted gamma radiation is in the
      <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
       ultraviolet
      </a>
      range.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beck_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Beck-22">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        [b]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the early history of the study of radioactivity, the different natural isotopes of thorium were given different names. In this scheme,
      <sup>
       227
      </sup>
      Th was named radioactinium (RdAc),
      <sup>
       228
      </sup>
      Th radiothorium (RdTh),
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th ionium (Io),
      <sup>
       231
      </sup>
      Th uranium Y (UY),
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th thorium (Th), and
      <sup>
       234
      </sup>
      Th uranium X1 (UX
      <sub>
       1
      </sub>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_15-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-15">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This reflects that
      <sup>
       227
      </sup>
      Th and
      <sup>
       231
      </sup>
      Th occur in the decay chain of natural
      <sup>
       235
      </sup>
      U (the actinium series),
      <sup>
       228
      </sup>
      Th occurs in the decay chain of
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th (the thorium series), and that
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th occurs in the decay chain of
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U (the uranium or radium series). The remaining natural thorium isotope,
      <sup>
       229
      </sup>
      Th, occurs in minute traces as part of the decay chain of
      <sup>
       237
      </sup>
      Np, the neptunium series, which is much less abundant than the other decay chains in nature and was hence discovered much later: therefore it does not have a historical name. When it was realised that all of these are isotopes of thorium, many of these names fell out of use, and "thorium" came to refer to all isotopes, not just
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_15-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-15">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The name ionium is still encountered for
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th in the context of
      <a href="/wiki/Ionium-thorium_dating" title="Ionium-thorium dating">
       ionium-thorium dating
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rafferty2010_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rafferty2010-27">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vertes2010_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-vertes2010-28">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Different isotopes of thorium behave identically chemically, but have slightly differing physical properties: for example, the densities of isotopically pure
      <sup>
       228
      </sup>
      Th,
      <sup>
       229
      </sup>
      Th,
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th, and
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th in g/cm
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      are respectively expected to be 11.524, 11.575, 11.626, and 11.727. (The discrepancy between this and the previously mentioned theoretical value of 11.724 is because this does not refer to isotopically pure thorium, but rather natural thorium, which as noted above is a mixture of mostly
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th but also a little
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th.)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-critical_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-critical-29">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The isotope
      <sup>
       229
      </sup>
      Th is expected to be
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fissionable" title="Fissionable">
       fissionable
      </a>
      with a bare
      <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">
       critical mass
      </a>
      of 2839 kg, although with steel reflectors this value could drop to 994 kg.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-critical_29-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-critical-29">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fissionable_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fissionable-30">
        [c]
       </a>
      </sup>
      While
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th is not fissionable, it is
      <a href="/wiki/Fertile_material" title="Fertile material">
       fertile
      </a>
      as it can be converted to fissile
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium-233" title="Uranium-233">
       <sup>
        233
       </sup>
       U
      </a>
      using
      <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">
       neutron capture
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-critical_29-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-critical-29">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Radiometric_dating">
       Radiometric dating
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Radiometric dating">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Two radiometric dating methods involve thorium isotopes:
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium-thorium_dating" title="Uranium-thorium dating">
       uranium-thorium dating
      </a>
      , involving the decay of
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium-234" title="Uranium-234">
       <sup>
        234
       </sup>
       U
      </a>
      to
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th (ionium), and ionium-thorium dating, which measures the ratio of
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th to
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th. These rely on the fact that
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th is a primordial radioisotope, but
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th only occurs as an intermediate decay product in the decay chain of
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uth_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-uth-32">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Uranium-thorium dating is a relatively short-range process because of the short half-lives of
      <sup>
       234
      </sup>
      U and
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th relative to the age of the Earth: it is also accompanied by a sister process involving the alpha decay of
      <sup>
       235
      </sup>
      U into
      <sup>
       231
      </sup>
      Th, which very quickly becomes the longer-lived
      <sup>
       231
      </sup>
      Pa, and this process is often used to check the results of uranium-thorium dating. Uranium-thorium dating is commonly used to determine the age of
      <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">
       calcium carbonate
      </a>
      materials such as
      <a href="/wiki/Speleothem" title="Speleothem">
       speleothem
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">
       coral
      </a>
      , because while uranium is rather soluble in water, thorium and protactinium are not, and so they are selectively precipitated into ocean-floor
      <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">
       sediments
      </a>
      , from which their ratios are measured. The scheme has a range of several hundred thousand years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uth_32-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-uth-32">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ionium-thorium dating is a related process, which exploits the insolubility of thorium (both
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th and
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th) and thus its presence in ocean sediments to date these sediments by measuring the ratio of
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th to
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rafferty2010_27-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-rafferty2010-27">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vertes2010_28-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-vertes2010-28">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Both of these dating methods assume that the proportion of
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th to
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th is a constant during the time period when the sediment layer was formed, that the sediment did not already contain thorium before contributions from the decay of uranium, and that the thorium cannot shift within the sediment layer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rafferty2010_27-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-rafferty2010-27">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vertes2010_28-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-vertes2010-28">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">
       Chemistry
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chemistry">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Compounds_of_thorium" title="Compounds of thorium">
       Compounds of thorium
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thorium_reactions.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1028" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thorium_reactions.svg/350px-Thorium_reactions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thorium_reactions.svg/525px-Thorium_reactions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thorium_reactions.svg/700px-Thorium_reactions.svg.png 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thorium_reactions.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Reactions of thorium metal
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A thorium atom has 90 electrons, of which four are
      <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">
       valence electrons
      </a>
      . Three
      <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">
       atomic orbitals
      </a>
      are theoretically available for the valence electrons to occupy: 5f, 6d, and 7s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder5960_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder5960-34">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite thorium's position in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-block" title="F-block">
       f-block
      </a>
      of the periodic table, it has an anomalous [Rn]6d
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      7s
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      electron configuration in the ground state, as the 5f and 6d subshells in the early actinides are very close in energy, even more so than the 4f and 5d subshells of the lanthanides: thorium's 6d subshells are lower in energy than its 5f subshells, because its 5f subshells are not well-shielded by the filled 6s and 6p subshells and are destabilised. Such unusual behaviour is due to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_effects" title="Relativistic effects">
       relativistic effects
      </a>
      , which are increasingly stronger near the bottom of the periodic table, specifically the relativistic
      <a href="/wiki/Spin%E2%80%93orbit_interaction" title="Spin–orbit interaction">
       spin–orbit interaction
      </a>
      . The closeness in energy levels of the 5f, 6d, and 7s energy levels of thorium result in thorium almost always losing all four of its valence electrons and hence occurring in its highest possible oxidation state of +4. This behaviour is quite distinct from that of its lanthanide congener cerium, for whom +4 is the highest possible state but +3 also plays an important role and is more stable. Therefore, thorium is much more similar to the
      <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">
       transition metals
      </a>
      zirconium and hafnium than to its true lanthanide congener cerium in its properties such as ionisation energies and redox potentials, and hence also in its chemistry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CottonSA2006_11-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CottonSA2006-11">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NIST-35">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Tetravalent thorium compounds are usually colourless or yellow, like those of
      <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">
       silver
      </a>
      or lead, as the Th
      <sup>
       4+
      </sup>
      ion has no 5f or 6d electrons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_7-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-7">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thorium and uranium are the most investigated of the radioactive elements because their radioactivity is slight enough to not pose major problems of handling and accessibility.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1262_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1262-36">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reactivity">
       Reactivity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Reactivity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Thorium is a highly
      <a href="/wiki/Reactivity_(chemistry)" title="Reactivity (chemistry)">
       reactive
      </a>
      and electropositive metal. Finely divided thorium metal presents a fire hazard due to its
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrophoric" title="Pyrophoric">
       pyrophoricity
      </a>
      and must therefore be handled carefully.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      When heated in air, thorium
      <a href="/wiki/Swarf" title="Swarf">
       turnings
      </a>
      ignite and burn brilliantly with a white light to produce the dioxide. In bulk, the reaction of pure thorium with air is slow, although corrosion may eventually occur after several months; most thorium samples are contaminated with varying degrees of the dioxide, which greatly accelerates corrosion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Such samples slowly tarnish in air, becoming grey and finally black at the surface.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard temperature and pressure">
       standard temperature and pressure
      </a>
      , thorium is slowly attacked by water, but does not readily dissolve in most common acids, with the exception of
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">
       hydrochloric acid
      </a>
      , where it dissolves leaving behind a black insoluble residue, probably ThO(OH,Cl)H.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CRC-37">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It dissolves in concentrated
      <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">
       nitric acid
      </a>
      containing a small amount of catalytic
      <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">
       fluoride
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorosilicate" title="Fluorosilicate">
       fluorosilicate
      </a>
      ions;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ekhyde_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ekhyde-38">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      if these are not present,
      <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">
       passivation
      </a>
      can occur, similarly to uranium and plutonium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1264_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1264-39">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Inorganic_compounds">
       Inorganic compounds
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Inorganic compounds">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CaF2_polyhedra.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="945" data-file-width="1417" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/CaF2_polyhedra.png/250px-CaF2_polyhedra.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/CaF2_polyhedra.png/375px-CaF2_polyhedra.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/CaF2_polyhedra.png/500px-CaF2_polyhedra.png 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CaF2_polyhedra.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Thorium dioxide has the
        <a href="/wiki/Fluorite" title="Fluorite">
         fluorite
        </a>
        structure. Th
        <sup>
         4+
        </sup>
        :
        <span style="color:silver; background:silver;">
         __
        </span>
        /  O
        <sup>
         2−
        </sup>
        :
        <span style="color:#9c0; background:#9c0;">
         __
        </span>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Most binary compounds of thorium with nonmetals may simply be prepared by heating the elements together.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1267_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1267-40">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In air, thorium burns to form the simple dioxide, ThO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      , also called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thoria" title="Thoria">
       thoria
      </a>
      or thorina: this has the
      <a href="/wiki/Fluorite" title="Fluorite">
       fluorite
      </a>
      structure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yamashita_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Yamashita-41">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thoria, a refractory material, has the highest
      <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">
       melting point
      </a>
      (3390 °C) of all known oxides.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Emsley-42">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is somewhat
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" title="Hygroscopic">
       hygroscopic
      </a>
      and reacts readily with water and many gases,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder7077_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7077-43">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      but dissolves easily in concentrated nitric acid in the presence of fluoride.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1269_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1269-44">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      When heated, it emits intense blue light through
      <a href="/wiki/Incandescence" title="Incandescence">
       incandescence
      </a>
      , which becomes white when mixed with its lighter homologue
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cerium_dioxide" title="Cerium dioxide">
       cerium dioxide
      </a>
      (CeO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      , ceria): this is the basis for its previously common application in gas mantles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder7077_43-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7077-43">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Several binary thorium
      <a href="/wiki/Chalcogen" title="Chalcogen">
       chalcogenides
      </a>
      and oxychalcogenides are also known with
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">
       sulfur
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">
       selenium
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">
       tellurium
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder9597_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder9597-45">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kristallstruktur_Uran(IV)-fluorid.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1100" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Kristallstruktur_Uran%28IV%29-fluorid.png/220px-Kristallstruktur_Uran%28IV%29-fluorid.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Kristallstruktur_Uran%28IV%29-fluorid.png/330px-Kristallstruktur_Uran%28IV%29-fluorid.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Kristallstruktur_Uran%28IV%29-fluorid.png/440px-Kristallstruktur_Uran%28IV%29-fluorid.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kristallstruktur_Uran(IV)-fluorid.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Crystal structure of thorium tetrafluoride. Th
        <sup>
         4+
        </sup>
        :
        <span style="color:silver; background:silver;">
         __
        </span>
        /  F
        <sup>
         −
        </sup>
        :
        <span style="color:#9c0; background:#9c0;">
         __
        </span>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      All four thorium tetrahalides are known, as are some low-valent bromides and iodides:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-46">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the tetrahalides are all 8-coordinated hygroscopic compounds that dissolve easily in polar solvents such as water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1271_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1271-47">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Additionally, many related polyhalide ions are also known.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_46-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-46">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thorium tetrafluoride has a
      <a href="/wiki/Monoclinic_crystal_system" title="Monoclinic crystal system">
       monoclinic
      </a>
      crystal structure and is isotypic with
      <a href="/wiki/Zirconium_tetrafluoride" title="Zirconium tetrafluoride">
       zirconium tetrafluoride
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hafnium_tetrafluoride" title="Hafnium tetrafluoride">
       hafnium tetrafluoride
      </a>
      , where the Th
      <sup>
       4+
      </sup>
      ions are coordinated with F
      <sup>
       −
      </sup>
      ions in somewhat distorted
      <a href="/wiki/Square_antiprism" title="Square antiprism">
       square antiprisms
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_46-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-46">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The other tetrahalides instead have dodecahedral geometry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1271_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1271-47">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lower iodides ThI
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      (black) and ThI
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      (gold) can also be prepared by reducing the tetraiodide with thorium metal: These do not contain Th(III) and Th(II), but instead contain Th
      <sup>
       4+
      </sup>
      and could be more clearly formulated as
      <a href="/wiki/Electride" title="Electride">
       electride
      </a>
      compounds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_46-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-46">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many polynary halides with the alkali metals,
      <a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">
       barium
      </a>
      , thallium, and ammonium are known for thorium fluorides, chlorides, and bromides.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder7894_46-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7894-46">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, when treated with
      <a href="/wiki/Potassium_fluoride" title="Potassium fluoride">
       potassium fluoride
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">
       hydrofluoric acid
      </a>
      , Th
      <sup>
       4+
      </sup>
      forms the complex anion
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       ThF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        2−
        <br/>
        6
       </span>
      </span>
      , which precipitates as an insoluble salt, K
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      ThF
      <sub>
       6
      </sub>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ekhyde_38-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ekhyde-38">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thorium borides, carbides, silicides, and nitrides are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refractory_material" title="Refractory material">
       refractory materials
      </a>
      , as are those of uranium and plutonium, and have thus received attention as possible
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">
       nuclear fuels
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1267_40-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1267-40">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      All four heavier
      <a href="/wiki/Pnictogen" title="Pnictogen">
       pnictogens
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">
       phosphorus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">
       arsenic
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">
       antimony
      </a>
      , and bismuth) also form binary thorium compounds. Thorium germanides are also known.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder97101_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder97101-48">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thorium reacts with hydrogen to form the thorium hydrides ThH
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      and Th
      <sub>
       4
      </sub>
      H
      <sub>
       15
      </sub>
      , the latter of which is superconducting below the transition temperature of 7.5–8 K; at standard temperature and pressure, it conducts electricity like a metal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6466_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6466-49">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      They are thermally unstable and readily decompose upon exposure to air or moisture.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1267_40-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1267-40">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Coordination_compounds">
       Coordination compounds
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Coordination compounds">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In acidic aqueous solution, thorium occurs as the tetrapositive
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aqua_ion" title="Aqua ion">
       aqua ion
      </a>
      [Th(H
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      O)
      <sub>
       9
      </sub>
      ]
      <sup>
       4+
      </sup>
      , which has
      <a href="/wiki/Tricapped_trigonal_prismatic_molecular_geometry" title="Tricapped trigonal prismatic molecular geometry">
       tricapped trigonal prismatic molecular geometry
      </a>
      :
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder117134_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder117134-50">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      at pH &lt; 3, the solutions of thorium salts are dominated by this cation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder117134_50-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder117134-50">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Th
      <sup>
       4+
      </sup>
      ion is the largest of the tetrapositive actinide ions, and depending on the coordination number can have a radius between 0.95 and 1.14 Å.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder117134_50-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder117134-50">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is quite acidic due to its high charge, slightly stronger than
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfurous_acid" title="Sulfurous acid">
       sulfurous acid
      </a>
      : thus it tends to undergo hydrolysis and polymerisation (though to a lesser extent than
      <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
       Fe
       <sup>
        3+
       </sup>
      </a>
      ), predominantly to [Th
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      (OH)
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      ]
      <sup>
       6+
      </sup>
      in solutions with pH 3 or below, but in more alkaline solution polymerisation continues until the gelatinous hydroxide Th(OH)
      <sub>
       4
      </sub>
      is formed and precipitates out (though equilibrium may take weeks to be reached, because the polymerisation usually slows down significantly just before the precipitation). This is different from the behaviour of Zr
      <sup>
       4+
      </sup>
      and Hf
      <sup>
       4+
      </sup>
      , which do not hydrolyse appreciably in aqueous solution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1275_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1275-52">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As a
      <a href="/wiki/HSAB_theory" title="HSAB theory">
       hard Lewis acid
      </a>
      , Th
      <sup>
       4+
      </sup>
      favours hard ligands with oxygen atoms as donors: complexes with sulfur atoms as donors are less stable and are more prone to hydrolysis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CottonSA2006_11-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-CottonSA2006-11">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Large coordination numbers are the rule for thorium due to its large size. Thorium nitrate pentahydrate was the first known example of coordination number 11, the oxalate tetrahydrate has coordination number 10, and the borohydride (first prepared in the
      <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">
       Manhattan Project
      </a>
      ) has coordination number 14.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1275_52-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1275-52">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The distinctive ability of thorium salts is their high solubility, not only in water, but also in polar organic solvents.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_7-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-7">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Many other inorganic thorium compounds with polyatomic anions are known, such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Perchlorate" title="Perchlorate">
       perchlorates
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">
       sulfates
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfite" title="Sulfite">
       sulfites
      </a>
      , nitrates, carbonates, phosphates,
      <a href="/wiki/Vanadate" title="Vanadate">
       vanadates
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Molybdate" title="Molybdate">
       molybdates
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromate" title="Chromate">
       chromates
      </a>
      , and their hydrated forms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder101115_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder101115-53">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      They are important in thorium purification and the disposal of nuclear waste, but most of them have not yet been fully characterised, especially regarding their structural properties.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder101115_53-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder101115-53">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, thorium nitrate is produced by reacting thorium hydroxide with nitric acid: it is soluble in water and alcohols and is an important intermediate in the purification of thorium and its compounds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder101115_53-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder101115-53">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thorium complexes with organic ligands, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Oxalate" title="Oxalate">
       oxalate
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citrate" title="Citrate">
       citrate
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EDTA" title="EDTA">
       EDTA
      </a>
      , are much stronger and tend to occur naturally in natural thorium-containing waters in concentrations orders of magnitude higher than the inorganic complexes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder117134_50-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder117134-50">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Organothorium_compounds">
       Organothorium compounds
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Organothorium compounds">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
