      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thorium_half_sandwich.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="940" data-file-width="1185" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Thorium_half_sandwich.svg/120px-Thorium_half_sandwich.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Thorium_half_sandwich.svg/180px-Thorium_half_sandwich.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Thorium_half_sandwich.svg/240px-Thorium_half_sandwich.svg.png 2x" width="120"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thorium_half_sandwich.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Piano-stool structure of (
        <i>
         η
        </i>
        <sup>
         8
        </sup>
        -C
        <sub>
         8
        </sub>
        H
        <sub>
         8
        </sub>
        )ThCl
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
        (THF)
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Most of the work on organothorium compounds has focused on the
      <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentadienyl" title="Cyclopentadienyl">
       cyclopentadienyls
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cyclooctatetraenide_anion" title="Cyclooctatetraenide anion">
       cyclooctatetraenyls
      </a>
      . Like many of the early and middle actinides (up to
      <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">
       americium
      </a>
      , and also expected for
      <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">
       curium
      </a>
      ), thorium forms the yellow cyclooctatetraenide complex Th(C
      <sub>
       8
      </sub>
      H
      <sub>
       8
      </sub>
      )
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      , thorocene. It is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotypic" title="Isotypic">
       isotypic
      </a>
      with the better-known analogous uranium compound,
      <a href="/wiki/Uranocene" title="Uranocene">
       uranocene
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder116117_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder116117-54">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It can be prepared by reacting
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potassium_cyclooctatetraenide" title="Potassium cyclooctatetraenide">
       K
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       C
       <sub>
        8
       </sub>
       H
       <sub>
        8
       </sub>
      </a>
      with thorium tetrachloride in
      <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrofuran" title="Tetrahydrofuran">
       tetrahydrofuran
      </a>
      (THF) at the temperature of
      <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">
       dry ice
      </a>
      , or by reacting thorium tetrafluoride with MgC
      <sub>
       8
      </sub>
      H
      <sub>
       8
      </sub>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder116117_54-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder116117-54">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is an unstable compound in air and outright decomposes in water or at 190 °C.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder116117_54-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder116117-54">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Half-sandwich compounds are also known, such as (
      <i>
       η
      </i>
      <sup>
       8
      </sup>
      -C
      <sub>
       8
      </sub>
      H
      <sub>
       8
      </sub>
      )ThCl
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      (THF)
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      , which has a piano-stool structure and is made by reacting thorocene with thorium tetrachloride in tetrahydrofuran.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CottonSA2006_11-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-CottonSA2006-11">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The simplest of the cyclopentadienyls are Th(C
      <sub>
       5
      </sub>
      H
      <sub>
       5
      </sub>
      )
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      and Th(C
      <sub>
       5
      </sub>
      H
      <sub>
       5
      </sub>
      )
      <sub>
       4
      </sub>
      : many derivatives are known. The former (which has two forms, one purple and one green) is a rare example of thorium in the formal +3 oxidation state;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder116117_54-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder116117-54">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1278_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1278-55">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
      a formal +2 oxidation state even occurs in a derivative.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The chloride derivative [Th(C
      <sub>
       5
      </sub>
      H
      <sub>
       5
      </sub>
      )
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      Cl] is prepared by heating thorium tetrachloride with
      <a href="/wiki/Limiting_reagent" title="Limiting reagent">
       limiting
      </a>
      K(C
      <sub>
       5
      </sub>
      H
      <sub>
       5
      </sub>
      ) used (other univalent metal cyclopentadienyls can also be used). The
      <a href="/wiki/Alkyl" title="Alkyl">
       alkyl
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Aryl" title="Aryl">
       aryl
      </a>
      derivatives are prepared from the chloride derivative and have received attention due to the insight they give regarding the nature of the Th–C
      <a href="/wiki/Sigma_bond" title="Sigma bond">
       sigma bond
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1278_55-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1278-55">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other organothorium compounds are not well-studied. Tetrabenzylthorium, Th(CH
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      C
      <sub>
       6
      </sub>
      H
      <sub>
       5
      </sub>
      ), and tetraallylthorium, Th(C
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      H
      <sub>
       5
      </sub>
      )
      <sub>
       4
      </sub>
      , are known, but their structures have not yet been determined and they decompose slowly at room temperature. Thorium forms the monocapped trigonal prismatic anion [Th(CH
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      )
      <sub>
       7
      </sub>
      ]
      <sup>
       3−
      </sup>
      , heptamethylthorate, which forms the salt [Li(tmeda)]
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      [ThMe
      <sub>
       7
      </sub>
      ] (tmeda = Me
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      NCH
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      CH
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      NMe
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      ). Although one methyl group is only attached to the thorium atom (Th–C distance 257.1 pm) and the other six connect the lithium and thorium atoms (Th–C distances 265.5–276.5 pm) they behave equivalently in solution. Tetramethylthorium, Th(CH
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      )
      <sub>
       4
      </sub>
      , is not known, but its
      <a href="/wiki/Adduct" title="Adduct">
       adducts
      </a>
      are stabilised by
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">
       phosphine
      </a>
      ligands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CottonSA2006_11-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-CottonSA2006-11">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">
       Occurrence
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Occurrence">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Occurrence_of_thorium" title="Occurrence of thorium">
       Occurrence of thorium
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">
       Formation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Formation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th is a primordial nuclide, having existed in its current form for over ten billion years; it was forged in the cores of dying stars through the
      <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">
       r-process
      </a>
      and scattered across the galaxy by
      <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">
       supernovae
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cameron_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cameron-57">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The letter "r" stands for "rapid neutron capture", and occurs in core-collapse supernovae, where heavy seed nuclei such as
      <a href="/wiki/Iron-56" title="Iron-56">
       <sup>
        56
       </sup>
       Fe
      </a>
      rapidly capture neutrons, running up against the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_drip_line" title="Neutron drip line">
       neutron drip line
      </a>
      , as neutrons are captured much faster than the resulting nuclides can
      <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">
       beta decay
      </a>
      back toward stability. Neutron capture is the only way for stars to synthesise elements beyond iron because of the increased
      <a href="/wiki/Coulomb_barrier" title="Coulomb barrier">
       Coulomb barriers
      </a>
      that make interactions between charged particles difficult at high atomic numbers and the fact that fusion beyond
      <sup>
       56
      </sup>
      Fe is endothermic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nucleosynthesis_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nucleosynthesis-58">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because of the abrupt loss of stability past
      <sup>
       209
      </sup>
      Bi, the r-process is the only process of stellar nucleosynthesis that can create isotopes of thorium and uranium, because all other processes are too slow and the intermediate nuclei alpha decay before they capture enough neutrons to reach these elements.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cameron_57-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cameron-57">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B2FH_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-B2FH-59">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="center">
      <div class="thumb tnone">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:1102px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elements_abundance-bars.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="3000" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Elements_abundance-bars.svg/1100px-Elements_abundance-bars.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Elements_abundance-bars.svg/1650px-Elements_abundance-bars.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Elements_abundance-bars.svg/2200px-Elements_abundance-bars.svg.png 2x" width="1100"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elements_abundance-bars.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Estimated abundances of the 83 primordial elements in the Solar system. Thorium is one of the rarest elements.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In the universe, thorium is among the rarest of the primordial elements: it achieves this position not only because it is one of the two elements that can be produced only in the r-process (with the other one being uranium), but also because it has slowly been decaying away from the moment it formed. The only primordial elements rarer than thorium are
      <a href="/wiki/Thulium" title="Thulium">
       thulium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lutetium" title="Lutetium">
       lutetium
      </a>
      , tantalum, and rhenium, the odd-numbered elements just before the third peak of r-process abundances around the heavy platinum group metals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cameron_57-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cameron-57">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nucleosynthesis_58-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nucleosynthesis-58">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [d]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Furthermore, neutron capture by nuclides beyond the end of the line of beta stability at
      <i>
       A
      </i>
      = 209 often results in nuclear fission instead of neutron absorption, reducing the fraction of nuclei that cross the gap of instability past bismuth to become thorium. On Earth, thorium is much more abundant: with an abundance of 8.1 ppm in the Earth's crust, it is one of the most abundant of the heavy elements, almost as abundant as lead (13 ppm) and significantly more abundant than tin (2.1 ppm).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1294_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1294-62">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is because thorium, being a
      <a href="/wiki/Goldschmidt_classification" title="Goldschmidt classification">
       lithophile
      </a>
      , is likely to form oxide minerals that do not sink into the core. On the other hand, the heavy platinum group metals have no affinity for oxygen and their oxides may even be thermodynamically unstable with respect to the elements. Instead, readily dissolve in iron and sink down with it into the core. Furthermore, thorium compounds are also poorly soluble in water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-albarede_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-albarede-63">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="On_Earth">
       On Earth
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: On Earth">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="653" data-file-width="899" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg/350px-Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg/525px-Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg/700px-Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg.png 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiogenic_heat" title="Radiogenic heat">
         radiogenic heat
        </a>
        from the decay of
        <sup>
         232
        </sup>
        Th (violet) is a major contributor to the
        <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_internal_heat_budget" title="Earth's internal heat budget">
         earth's internal heat budget
        </a>
        . The other major contributors are
        <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">
         <sup>
          235
         </sup>
         U
        </a>
        (red),
        <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">
         <sup>
          238
         </sup>
         U
        </a>
        (green), and
        <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40">
         <sup>
          40
         </sup>
         K
        </a>
        (yellow).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Natural thorium is essentially isotopically pure
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th, which is the longest-lived and most stable isotope of thorium, having a half-life comparable to the age of the universe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_15-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-15">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Its
      <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">
       radioactive decay
      </a>
      produces a significant amount of the
      <a href="/wiki/Earth#Heat" title="Earth">
       Earth's internal heat
      </a>
      ; the other major contributors are the shorter-lived primordial nuclides,
      <sup>
       235
      </sup>
      U,
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U, and
      <sup>
       40
      </sup>
      K.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGJuly11_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NGJuly11-64">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The other natural thorium isotopes are much shorter-lived; of them, only
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th is usually detectable, occurring in
      <a href="/wiki/Secular_equilibrium" title="Secular equilibrium">
       secular equilibrium
      </a>
      with its parent
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U, and making up at most 0.04% of natural thorium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_15-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-15">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [e]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On Earth, thorium is not a rare element as was previously thought, having a crustal abundance comparable to that of lead and
      <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">
       molybdenum
      </a>
      , twice that of arsenic, and thrice that of tin.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-67">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thorium only occurs as a minor constituent of most minerals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_67-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-67">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Soil normally contains about 6
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">
       parts per million
      </a>
      (ppm) of thorium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In nature, thorium occurs in the +4 oxidation state, together with uranium(IV),
      <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">
       zirconium
      </a>
      (IV), hafnium(IV), and cerium(IV), but also with scandium, yttrium, and the trivalent lanthanides which have similar
      <a href="/wiki/Ionic_radius" title="Ionic radius">
       ionic radii
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_67-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-67">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because of thorium's radioactivity, minerals containing significant quantities of thorium are often
      <a href="/wiki/Metamictization" title="Metamictization">
       metamict
      </a>
      , their crystal structure having been partially or totally destroyed by the alpha radiation produced in the radioactive decay of thorium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodhead_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Woodhead-69">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An extreme example is
      <a href="/wiki/Ekanite" title="Ekanite">
       ekanite
      </a>
      , (Ca,Fe,Pb)
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      (Th,U)Si
      <sub>
       8
      </sub>
      O
      <sub>
       20
      </sub>
      , which almost never occurs in nonmetamict form due to thorium being an essential part of its chemical composition.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ekanite_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ekanite-70">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Monazite" title="Monazite">
       Monazite
      </a>
      is the most important commercial source of thorium because it occurs in large deposits worldwide, principally in
      <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
       India
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">
       South Africa
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
       Brazil
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
       Australia
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">
       Malaysia
      </a>
      . It contains around 2.5% thorium on average, although some deposits may contain up to 20% thorium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_67-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-67">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1255_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1255-71">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Monazite is a chemically unreactive
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">
       phosphate
      </a>
      mineral that is found as yellow or brown sand; its low reactivity makes it difficult to extract thorium from it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_67-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-67">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Allanite" title="Allanite">
       Allanite
      </a>
      can have 0.1–2% thorium and
      <a href="/wiki/Zircon" title="Zircon">
       zircon
      </a>
      up to 0.4% thorium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_67-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-67">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thorium dioxide occurs as the rare mineral
      <a href="/wiki/Thorianite" title="Thorianite">
       thorianite
      </a>
      , which usually contains up to 12% ThO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      . Due to its being isotypic with
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium_dioxide" title="Uranium dioxide">
       uranium dioxide
      </a>
      , these two common actinide dioxides can form solid-state solutions and the name of the mineral changes according to the ThO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      content.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_67-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-67">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [f]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thorite, or
      <a href="/wiki/Tetragonal_crystal_system" title="Tetragonal crystal system">
       tetragonal
      </a>
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium_silicate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Thorium silicate (page does not exist)">
       thorium silicate
      </a>
      (ThSiO
      <sub>
       4
      </sub>
      ), also has a high thorium content and is the mineral in which thorium was first discovered.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_67-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-67">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In thorium silicate minerals, the Th
      <sup>
       4+
      </sup>
      and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        4−
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      ions are often replaced with M
      <sup>
       3+
      </sup>
      (M = Sc, Y, Ln) and phosphate (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       PO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        3−
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      ) ions respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_67-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-67">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because of the great insolubility of thorium dioxide, thorium does not usually spread quickly through the environment when released in significant quantities: the Th
      <sup>
       4+
      </sup>
      ion is soluble, especially in acidic soils, and in such conditions the thorium concentration can reach 40 ppm.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4430" data-file-width="3063" height="318" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Tor's Fight with the Giants
        </i>
        (1872) by
        <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge" title="Mårten Eskil Winge">
         Mårten Eskil Winge
        </a>
        , depicting an artist's perception of
        <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">
         Thor
        </a>
        , the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_god" title="Norse god">
         Norse god
        </a>
        of thunder, raising
        <a href="/wiki/Mj%C3%B6lnir" title="Mjölnir">
         his hammer
        </a>
        in a battle against the
        <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6tunn" title="Jötunn">
         giants
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
         <a href="#cite_note-74">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1815, the Swedish chemist
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jakob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jakob Berzelius">
       Jöns Jakob Berzelius
      </a>
      analysed an unusual sample of
      <a href="/wiki/Gadolinite" title="Gadolinite">
       gadolinite
      </a>
      from a copper mine in
      <a href="/wiki/Falun" title="Falun">
       Falun
      </a>
      , central Sweden. He noted impregnated traces of a white mineral, which he cautiously assumed to be an earth (
      <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">
       oxide
      </a>
      in modern chemical nomenclature) of an unknown element. (By that time, Berzelius had already discovered two elements, cerium and selenium, but he had made a public mistake once, announcing a new element,
      <i>
       gahnium
      </i>
      , that turned out to be simply
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">
       zinc oxide
      </a>
      .)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lost_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lost-75">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Berzelius privately named the supposed tentative element "thorium" in 1817
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and its supposed oxide "thorina" after
      <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">
       Thor
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_god" title="Norse god">
       Norse god
      </a>
      of thunder.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1824, after more deposits of the same mineral in Vest-Agder, Norway, were discovered, he retracted his findings, as the mineral in question proved to actually be an
      <a href="/wiki/Yttrium" title="Yttrium">
       yttrium
      </a>
      mineral, primarily composed of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yttrium_phosphate" title="Yttrium phosphate">
       yttrium orthophosphate
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lost_75-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lost-75">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mindat_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mindat-79">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the yttrium in this mineral was initially mistaken as being a new element, the mineral was named kenotime by the French mineralogist
      <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Sulpice_Beudant" title="François Sulpice Beudant">
       François Sulpice Beudant
      </a>
      as a rebuke of Berzelius, from the
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
       Greek
      </a>
      words κενός (vain) and τιμή (honour). This became "
      <a href="/wiki/Xenotime" title="Xenotime">
       xenotime
      </a>
      " as a misprint from the beginning, blunting the criticism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lost_75-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lost-75">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mindat_79-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mindat-79">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handbook_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Handbook-80">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This misspelled form was later explained as being from ξενός (stranger to) and τιμή (honour), supposedly referencing the small, rare and easily overlooked crystals that xenotime occurs as.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1828,
      <a href="/wiki/Morten_Thrane_Esmark" title="Morten Thrane Esmark">
       Morten Thrane Esmark
      </a>
      found a black mineral on
      <a href="/wiki/L%C3%B8v%C3%B8ya,_Telemark" title="Løvøya, Telemark">
       Løvøya
      </a>
      island,
      <a href="/wiki/Telemark" title="Telemark">
       Telemark
      </a>
      county, Norway. He was a Norwegian
      <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">
       priest
      </a>
      and amateur
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mineralogist" title="Mineralogist">
       mineralogist
      </a>
      who studied the minerals in Telemark, where he served as
      <a href="/wiki/Vicar" title="Vicar">
       vicar
      </a>
      . He commonly sent the most interesting specimens, such as this one, to his father,
      <a href="/wiki/Jens_Esmark" title="Jens Esmark">
       Jens Esmark
      </a>
      , a noted mineralogist and professor of mineralogy and geology at the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oslo" title="University of Oslo">
       University of Oslo
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snl_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-snl-82">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The elder Esmark determined that it was not any known mineral and sent a sample to Berzelius for examination. Berzelius determined that it contained a new element.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He published his findings in 1829, having isolated an impure sample for the first time by reducing KThF
      <sub>
       5
      </sub>
      with
      <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">
       potassium
      </a>
      metal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Weeks-83">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Berzelius reused the name of the previous supposed element discovery.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks_83-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Weeks-83">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thus, he named the source mineral
      <a href="/wiki/Thorite" title="Thorite">
       thorite
      </a>
      , which has the chemical composition (Th,U)
      <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">
       SiO
       <sub>
        4
       </sub>
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Berzelius also made some initial characterisation of the new metal and its chemical compounds: he correctly determined that the thorium–oxygen mass ratio was 7.5 (its actual value is close to that, ~7.3), but he assumed the new element was divalent rather than tetravalent, and as such assumed that the atomic mass was 7.5 times that of oxygen (120
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">
       amu
      </a>
      ), while it is actually 15 times as large.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        [g]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He determined that thorium was a very electropositive metal, that he placed ahead of cerium and behind zirconium in electropositivity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leach2_88-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-leach2-88">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Berzelius did not isolate the element in its metallic state; for the first time, thorium was isolated in 1914 by D. Lely Jr. and L. Hamburger. They obtained 99% pure thorium metal by reducing thorium chloride with sodium metal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meister_89-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Meister-89">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [h]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A simpler method leading to even higher purity was discovered in 1927 by Marden and Rentschler, involving the reduction of thorium oxide with calcium when calcium chloride was present.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meister_89-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Meister-89">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">
       Dmitri Mendeleev
      </a>
      's 1869 periodic table, thorium and the
      <a href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" title="Rare earth element">
       rare earth elements
      </a>
      were placed outside the main body of the table, at the end of each vertical period after the
      <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">
       alkaline earth metals
      </a>
      . This reflected the belief at that time that thorium and the rare earth metals were divalent. With the later recognition that the rare earths were mostly trivalent and thorium was tetravalent, Mendeleev moved cerium and thorium to group IV in 1871, which contained the modern
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_group" title="Carbon group">
       carbon group
      </a>
      (group 14),
      <a href="/wiki/Group_4_element" title="Group 4 element">
       titanium group
      </a>
      (group 4), cerium, and thorium, because their maximum oxidation state was +4.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leach_92-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-leach-92">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jensen_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jensen-93">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cerium was soon removed from the main body of the table and placed in a separate lanthanide series, while thorium remained with group 4 as it had similar properties to its supposed lighter congeners in that group, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">
       titanium
      </a>
      and zirconium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masterton_94-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Masterton-94">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [i]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although thorium was discovered in 1828, it had no applications until 1885, when
      <a href="/wiki/Carl_Auer_von_Welsbach" title="Carl Auer von Welsbach">
       Carl Auer von Welsbach
      </a>
      invented the
      <a href="/wiki/Gas_mantle" title="Gas mantle">
       gas mantle
      </a>
      , a portable source of light which produces light from the
      <a href="/wiki/Incandescence" title="Incandescence">
       incandescence
      </a>
      of very hot thorium oxide, heated to extremely high temperatures by burning gaseous fuels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      After 1885, many applications were found for thorium and its compounds, such as in ceramics, carbon arc lamps, heat-resistant crucibles, and as catalysts for industrial chemical reactions such as the oxidation of ammonia to nitric acid.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG/220px-Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG/330px-Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG/440px-Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_thorium_dioxide_gas_mantle_-_oblong_shape.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
         World War II
        </a>
        thorium dioxide gas mantle
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In the late 19th century onward, the
      <a href="/wiki/Atomic_theory" title="Atomic theory">
       atomic theory
      </a>
      underwent significant improvements, which shaped the further history of thorium. Thorium was first observed to be radioactive in 1898, independently, by the German chemist
      <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Carl_Schmidt" title="Gerhard Carl Schmidt">
       Gerhard Carl Schmidt
      </a>
      and later that year, the Polish-French physicist
      <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">
       Marie Curie
      </a>
      . It was the second element that was found to be radioactive, after the 1896 discovery of radioactivity in uranium by
      <a href="/wiki/Henri_Becquerel" title="Henri Becquerel">
       Henri Becquerel
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Between 1900 and 1903,
      <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">
       Ernest Rutherford
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Soddy" title="Frederick Soddy">
       Frederick Soddy
      </a>
      showed how thorium decayed at a fixed rate over time into a series of other elements. This observation led to the identification of
      <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">
       half-life
      </a>
      as one of the outcomes of the
      <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">
       alpha particle
      </a>
      experiments that led to their disintegration theory of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">
       radioactivity
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The biological effect of radiation was discovered in 1903;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the danger presented by radioactivity to health and environment was the reason thorium was phased out of use in applications that did not explicitly use the radioactivity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      By the 1930s, it has been widely acknowledged that thorium possesses a minor threat to human organisms in large quantities.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1922,
      <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">
       Niels Bohr
      </a>
      published a
      <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">
       theoretical model
      </a>
      of the atom and its electron orbitals, which soon gathered wide acceptance. The model indicated that the seventh row of the periodic table should also have f-shells filling before the d-shells that were filled in the transition elements, like the sixth row with the lanthanides preceding the 5d transition metals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leach_92-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-leach-92">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Such a second extra-long periodic table row, to accommodate known and undiscovered elements with an atomic weight greater than bismuth (thorium, protactinium, and uranium, for example), had been postulated as far back as 1892 by Henry Bassett, who considered thorium and uranium to be members of a second series of rare earths, and predicted that more elements would be found within this series. Most investigators considered on the basis of their chemical properties that these elements were analogous to the 5d transition elements hafnium, tantalum, and tungsten, and considered the existence of the lanthanides in the sixth row to be a one-off fluke. The existence of a second inner transition series, in the form of the actinides, was not accepted until similarities with the electron structures of the lanthanides had been established.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was only with the discovery of the first
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic_element" title="Transuranic element">
       transuranic elements
      </a>
      , which from plutonium onward have dominant +3 and +4 oxidation states (neptunium having +4 and +5 as dominant states and +7 as an unstable state), that it was realised that the actinides were indeed filling f-orbitals rather than d-orbitals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thus it was not until 1945, after
      <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">
       Glenn T. Seaborg
      </a>
      and his team had discovered the transuranic elements americium and curium, that Seaborg realised that thorium was the second member of the actinide series and was filling an f-block row, instead of being the heavier congener of
      <a href="/wiki/Hafnium" title="Hafnium">
       hafnium
      </a>
      and filling a fourth d-block row.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masterton_94-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Masterton-94">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Today, thorium's similarities to hafnium are still sometimes acknowledged by calling it a "pseudo group-4 element".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pershina_103-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pershina-103">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite thorium's radioactivity, the element has remained in use for a long time for applications not exploiting the effect as no suitable alternatives could be found. While a 1981 study estimated that a dose from using a thorium mantle every weekend would be safe for a person,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straightdope_105-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-straightdope-105">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
      this was not the case for people manufacturing the mantles (and thus contacting many) as well as soils around some factory sites.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A major shift occurred as late as in the 1990s, when most of these applications that do not depend on thorium's radioactivity declined quickly due to safety and environmental concerns as suitable safer replacements have been found.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furuta_107-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Furuta-107">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Due to concerns, some manufacturers have switched to other materials, such as yttrium, although these are usually either more expensive or less efficient. Other manufacturers continue to make thorium mantles, but moved their factories to
      <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">
       developing countries
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straightdope_105-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-straightdope-105">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As recently as 2007, some companies continued to manufacture and sell thorium mantles without giving adequate information about their radioactivity, with some even fraudulently claiming them to be non-radioactive while in reality using large quantities of thorium, up to 259 milligrams per mantle.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furuta_107-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Furuta-107">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poljanc_108-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Poljanc-108">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle#List_of_thorium-fueled_reactors" title="Thorium fuel cycle">
       Efforts
      </a>
      have been applied to initiate usage of thorium and its radioactivity as a power source; the earliest thorium-based reactor was made in India: the first core at the
      <a href="/wiki/Indian_Point_Energy_Center" title="Indian Point Energy Center">
       Indian Point Energy Center
      </a>
      in 1962.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        [100]
       </a>
      </sup>
      India has one of the largest supplies of thorium in the world but does not have much uranium used elsewhere, and targeted in the 1950s at achieving energy independence for the country with their
      <a href="/wiki/India%27s_three-stage_nuclear_power_programme" title="India's three-stage nuclear power programme">
       three-stage nuclear power programme
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        [101]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        [102]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On the other hand, in most countries, the progress staggered because uranium was relatively abundant and the progress of thorium-based reactors was therefore slow (in the 20th century, 3 reactors were opened in India and 12 elsewhere
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        [103]
       </a>
      </sup>
      ). Large-scale research was begun in 1996 by the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">
       International Atomic Energy Agency
      </a>
      (IAEA) to study the use of thorium reactors; a year later, the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">
       U.S. Energy Department
      </a>
      began their research on the matter. Nuclear scientist
      <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Radkowsky" title="Alvin Radkowsky">
       Alvin Radkowsky
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Tel_Aviv_University" title="Tel Aviv University">
       Tel Aviv University
      </a>
      in Israel, the head designer of the American first civilian
      <a href="/wiki/Shippingport_Atomic_Power_Station" title="Shippingport Atomic Power Station">
       nuclear power plant at Shippingport, Pennsylvania
      </a>
      whose third core bred thorium,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asme-landmark_113-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-asme-landmark-113">
        [104]
       </a>
      </sup>
      founded a consortium to develop thorium reactors, which included other companies:
      <a href="/wiki/Raytheon" title="Raytheon">
       Raytheon
      </a>
      Nuclear Inc. and
      <a href="/wiki/Brookhaven_National_Laboratory" title="Brookhaven National Laboratory">
       Brookhaven National Laboratory
      </a>
      in the U.S. and the
      <a href="/wiki/Kurchatov_Institute" title="Kurchatov Institute">
       Kurchatov Institute
      </a>
      in Russia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radkowsky_114-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Radkowsky-114">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the 21st century, thorium's potential for improving proliferation resistance and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" title="Nuclear waste">
       waste
      </a>
      characteristics led to renewed interest in the thorium fuel cycle.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        [107]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        [108]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Production">
       Production
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Production">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div style="float: right; margin: 2px; font-size:85%; margin-right:18px; margin-bottom:18px">
      <table cellpadding="3" class="wikitable sortable collapsible" rules="all" style="background:#f9f9f9; border:1px #aaa solid">
       <caption>
        <b>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USGS" title="USGS">
          USGS
         </a>
         estimates in
         <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">
          tonnes
         </a>
         , 2011
        </b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
         <a href="#cite_note-118">
          [109]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Country
        </th>
        <th data-sort-type="number">
         Reserves
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <span class="flagicon">
          <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="1235" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>

         </a>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <span class="flagicon">

         </a>

        <td align="right">

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <span class="flagicon">
          <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="2800" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Flag_of_Malaysia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Malaysia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Flag_of_Malaysia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Malaysia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Flag_of_Malaysia.svg/46px-Flag_of_Malaysia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>

         </a>

        <td align="right">
         4,500
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <span class="flagicon">

          South Africa
         </a>

        </td>
       </tr>

         Other countries
        </td>

         90,000
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td align="center">
         <i>
          World total
         </i>
        </td>
        <td align="right">
         1,400,000
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
     </div>
     <p>
      Thorium is extracted mostly from monazite: thorium
      <a href="/wiki/Pyrophosphate" title="Pyrophosphate">
       pyrophosphate
      </a>
      (ThP
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      O
      <sub>
       7
      </sub>
      ) is reacted with nitric acid, and the produced thorium nitrate treated with
      <a href="/wiki/Tributyl_phosphate" title="Tributyl phosphate">
       tributyl phosphate
      </a>
      . Rare-earth impurities are separated by increasing the pH in sulfate solution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g215_119-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-g215-119">
        [110]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In another extraction method, monazite is decomposed with a 45% aqueous solution of
      <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">
       sodium hydroxide
      </a>
      at 140 °C. Mixed metal hydroxides are extracted first, filtered at 80 °C, washed with water and dissolved with concentrated hydrochloric acid. Next, the acidic solution is neutralised with hydroxides to pH = 5.8 that results in precipitation of thorium hydroxide (Th(OH)
      <sub>
       4
      </sub>
      ) contaminated with ~3% of rare-earth hydroxides; the remaining rare-earth hydroxides remain in solution. Thorium hydroxide is dissolved in an inorganic acid and then purified from the rare earth elements. An efficient method is the dissolution of thorium hydroxide in nitric acid, because the resulting solution can be purified by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid-liquid_extraction" title="Liquid-liquid extraction">
       extraction
      </a>
      with organic solvents:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g215_119-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-g215-119">
        [110]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       Th(OH)
       <sub>
        4
       </sub>
       + 4 HNO
       <sub>
        3
       </sub>
       → Th(NO
       <sub>
        3
       </sub>
       )
       <sub>
        4
       </sub>
       + 4 H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       O
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Metallic thorium is separated from the anhydrous oxide or chloride by reacting it with calcium in an inert atmosphere:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1255_71-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1255-71">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       ThO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       + 2 Ca → 2 CaO + Th
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Sometimes thorium is extracted by
      <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">
       electrolysis
      </a>
      of a fluoride in a mixture of sodium and potassium chloride at 700–800 °C in a
      <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">
       graphite
      </a>
      crucible. Highly pure thorium can be extracted from its iodide with the
      <a href="/wiki/Crystal_bar_process" title="Crystal bar process">
       crystal bar process
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        [111]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Since thorium has few applications today, only a few hundred tonnes of it are produced each year, mostly as a by-product of the production of uranium and the lanthanides, and even these meagre quantities exceed the current demand for thorium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        [112]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Half of this thorium produced is used in gas mantles. Nevertheless, production could easily be increased, and probably would be if thorium became used in large quantities as nuclear fuel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1255_71-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1255-71">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Present knowledge of the distribution of thorium resources is poor because of the relatively low-key exploration efforts arising out of insignificant demand.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        [113]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">
       Applications
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Applications">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Many applications of thorium are becoming obsolete due to environmental concerns largely stemming from the radioactivity of thorium and its decay products. Thorium is thus being phased out of many of its uses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furuta_107-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Furuta-107">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thorium is used in
      <a href="/wiki/Gas_tungsten_arc_welding" title="Gas tungsten arc welding">
       gas tungsten arc welding
      </a>
      (GTAW) to increase the high-temperature strength of tungsten electrodes and improve arc stability.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWickleder200652_123-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWickleder200652-123">
        [114]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In electronic equipment, thorium coating of tungsten wire improves the electron
      <a href="/wiki/Thermionic_emission" title="Thermionic emission">
       emission
      </a>
      of heated
      <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">
       cathodes
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_37-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CRC-37">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thorium is added to tungsten because it lowers the effective
      <a href="/wiki/Work_function" title="Work function">
       work function
      </a>
      with the result that the thoriated tungsten
      <a href="/wiki/Hot_cathode" title="Hot cathode">
       thermocathode
      </a>
      emits electrons at considerably lower temperatures.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is used in thoriated tungsten elements found in the
      <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb#Filament" title="Incandescent light bulb">
       filaments
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">
       vacuum tubes
      </a>
      , e.g.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetron" title="Magnetron">
       magnetron
      </a>
      found in
      <a href="/wiki/Microwave_oven" title="Microwave oven">
       microwave oven
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Many applications of thorium use, rather than the metal, its dioxide, commonly called "thoria" in the industry. The melting point of thoria is 3300 °C – the highest of all known oxides; only few substances have higher melting points.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley_42-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Emsley-42">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This means that when heated to high temperatures,
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness">
         <span title="This information is too vague. (August 2016)">
          vague
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      it does not melt, but merely glows with an intense blue light; addition of cerium dioxide gives a bright white light.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder7077_43-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder7077-43">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sometimes thorium nitrate is used instead, because it will thermally decompose to thoria at high temperatures. This property of thoria is used in mantles of portable gas lights, including natural gas lamps, oil lamps and camping lights.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-appl_124-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-appl-124">
        [115]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Additionally, thoria is a material for
      <a href="/wiki/Refraction_(metallurgy)" title="Refraction (metallurgy)">
       heat-resistant
      </a>
      ceramics, as used in high-temperature laboratory
      <a href="/wiki/Crucible" title="Crucible">
       crucibles
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1702" data-file-width="4734" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg/220px-Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg/330px-Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg/440px-Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yellowing_of_thorium_lenses.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Yellowed thoria lens (left), a similar lens partially de-yellowed with ultraviolet radiation (centre), and lens without yellowing (right)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      When added to
      <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">
       glass
      </a>
      , thoria helps increase
      <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">
       refractive index
      </a>
      and decrease
      <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">
       dispersion
      </a>
      . Such glass finds application in high-quality
      <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">
       lenses
      </a>
      for cameras and scientific instruments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_37-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-CRC-37">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The radiation from these lenses can darken them and turn them yellow over a period of years and degrade film, but the health risks are minimal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        [116]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Yellowed lenses may be restored to their original colourless state with lengthy exposure to intense ultraviolet radiation.
     </p>
     <p>
      Thoria has been used as a chemical
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">
       catalyst
      </a>
      in the conversion of
      <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
       ammonia
      </a>
      to nitric acid,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">
       petroleum
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Cracking_(chemistry)" title="Cracking (chemistry)">
       cracking
      </a>
      and in producing
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">
       sulfuric acid
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Thorium tetrafluoride is used as an antireflection material in multilayered optical coatings. It has an optical transparency in the range of 0.35–12 µm, and its radiation is primarily due to alpha particles, which can be easily stopped by a thin cover layer of another material.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        [117]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thorium tetrafluoride was also used in manufacturing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_arc_lamp" title="Carbon arc lamp">
       carbon arc lamps
      </a>
      , which provided high-intensity illumination for movie projectors and search lights.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-appl_124-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-appl-124">
        [115]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_use_for_nuclear_energy">
       Potential use for nuclear energy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Potential use for nuclear energy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle" title="Thorium fuel cycle">
       Thorium fuel cycle
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_breeder_reactor" title="Thermal breeder reactor">
       thermal breeder reactors
      </a>
      , the fertile
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fissionable_30-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-fissionable-30">
        [c]
       </a>
      </sup>
      isotope
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th is bombarded by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slow_neutron" title="Slow neutron">
       slow neutrons
      </a>
      , undergoing neutron capture to become
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      Th, which undergoes two consecutive beta decays to become first
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protactinium-233" title="Protactinium-233">
       <sup>
        233
       </sup>
       Pa
      </a>
      and then the fissile
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

        Np
