     </table>
     <p>
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U is fissile and hence can be used as a nuclear fuel in much the same way as the more-commonly used
      <sup>
       235
      </sup>
      U or
      <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">
       <sup>
        239
       </sup>
       Pu
      </a>
      . When
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U undergoes nuclear fission, the neutrons emitted can strike further
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th nuclei, restarting the cycle.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This closely parallels the uranium fuel cycle in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_breeder_reactor" title="Fast breeder reactor">
       fast breeder reactors
      </a>
      where
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U undergoes neutron capture to become
      <sup>
       239
      </sup>
      U, beta decaying to first
      <sup>
       239
      </sup>
      Np and then fissile
      <sup>
       239
      </sup>
      Pu.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
       <a href="#cite_note-127">
        [118]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The main advantage of the thorium fuel cycle is that thorium is more abundant than uranium and hence can satisfy world energy demands for longer. Furthermore,
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th absorbs neutrons more readily than
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U, and not only does
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U have a higher probability of fission upon neutron capture (92.0%) than
      <sup>
       235
      </sup>
      U (85.5%) or
      <sup>
       239
      </sup>
      Pu (73.5%),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        [119]
       </a>
      </sup>
      it also releases more neutrons upon fission on average.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1259_129-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1259-129">
        [120]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An added advantage
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U and
      <sup>
       239
      </sup>
      Pu enjoy over all other fissile nuclei (except the naturally occurring
      <sup>
       235
      </sup>
      U) is that they can be
      <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">
       bred
      </a>
      from neutron capture by the naturally-occurring quantity isotopes
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th and
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ronen_130-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ronen-130">
        [121]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anucene_131-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-anucene-131">
        [122]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
       <a href="#cite_note-132">
        [j]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thorium fuels also result in a safer and better-performing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactor_core" title="Reactor core">
       reactor core
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      because thoria has a higher melting point, higher
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">
       thermal conductivity
      </a>
      , and lower
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_thermal_expansion" title="Coefficient of thermal expansion">
       coefficient of thermal expansion
      </a>
      than the now-common fuel uranium dioxide (UO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      ): thoria is also more
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_stability" title="Chemical stability">
       chemically stable
      </a>
      as, unlike uranium dioxide, it does not further oxidise to
      <a href="/wiki/Triuranium_octoxide" title="Triuranium octoxide">
       triuranium octoxide
      </a>
      (U
      <sub>
       3
      </sub>
      O
      <sub>
       8
      </sub>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thorium_Fuel_Cycle_.E2.80.93_Potential_Benefits_and_Challenges_133-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Thorium_Fuel_Cycle_.E2.80.93_Potential_Benefits_and_Challenges-133">
        [123]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Furthermore, while a single neutron capture by
      <sup>
       238
      </sup>
      U would produce transuranic waste, along with it fissile
      <sup>
       239
      </sup>
      Pu, five captures are generally necessary to do so from
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th. 98–99% of thorium-cycle fuel nuclei would fission at either
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U or
      <sup>
       235
      </sup>
      U, so fewer long-lived transuranics are produced. Because of this, thorium is a potentially attractive alternative to uranium in
      <a href="/wiki/MOX_fuel" title="MOX fuel">
       mixed oxide (MOX) fuels
      </a>
      to minimise the generation of transuranics and maximise the destruction of plutonium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wnn-20130621_134-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-wnn-20130621-134">
        [124]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The main disadvantage is that it is difficult and dangerous to reprocess the used fuel because many of the daughters of
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th and
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U are strong gamma emitters.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1259_129-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1259-129">
        [120]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Additionally, all
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U production methods other than mercury fluourescence always result in significant impurities of the very dangerous gamma emitter
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium-232" title="Uranium-232">
       <sup>
        232
       </sup>
       U
      </a>
      , either from parasitic (n,2n) reactions on
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th,
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      Pa, or
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U that result in the loss of a neutron, or from double neutron capture of
      <sup>
       230
      </sup>
      Th, an impurity in natural
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Intro2WMD_135-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Intro2WMD-135">
        [125]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         230
         <br/>
         90
        </span>
        Th
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </span>
       + n →
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         231
         <br/>
         90
        </span>
        Th
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </span>
       +
       <span class="texhtml">
        γ
       </span>
       <span class="sfrac nowrap;">
        <span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;">
         <span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;">
          <i>
           β
          </i>
          <sup>
           −
          </sup>
         </span>
         <span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">
          →
         </span>
         <span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">
          25.5 h
         </span>
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
          231
          <br/>
          91
         </span>
         Pa
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
         </span>
        </span>
        <span class="sfrac nowrap;">
         <span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;">
          <span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;">
           <i>
            α
           </i>
          </span>
          <span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">
           →
          </span>
          <span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">
           3.28 × 10
           <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
            4
            <br/>
           </span>
           y
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </span>
      </dd>
      <dd>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         231
         <br/>
         91
        </span>
        Pa
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </span>
       + n →
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         232
         <br/>
         91
        </span>
        Pa
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </span>
       +
       <span class="texhtml">
        γ
       </span>
       <span class="sfrac nowrap;">
        <span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;">
         <span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;">
          <i>
           β
          </i>
          <sup>
           −
          </sup>
         </span>
         <span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">
          →
         </span>
         <span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">
          1.3 d
         </span>
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
          232
          <br/>
          92
         </span>
         U
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
         </span>
        </span>
        <span class="sfrac nowrap;">
         <span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;">
          <span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;">
           <i>
            α
           </i>
          </span>
          <span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">
           →
          </span>
          <span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">
           69 y
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      These impurities of
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      U make
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U very easy to detect and very dangerous to work on, and the impracticality of their separation limits the possibilities of
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">
       nuclear proliferation
      </a>
      using
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U as the fissile material.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Intro2WMD_135-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Intro2WMD-135">
        [125]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Additionally,
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      Pa has a relatively long half-life of 27 days and a high
      <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(physics)" title="Cross section (physics)">
       cross section
      </a>
      for neutron capture. Thus it is a
      <a href="/wiki/Neutron_poison" title="Neutron poison">
       neutron poison
      </a>
      : instead of rapidly decaying to the useful
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U, a significant amount of
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      Pa converts to
      <sup>
       234
      </sup>
      U and consumes neutrons, degrading
      <a href="/wiki/Neutron_economy" title="Neutron economy">
       the reactor efficiency
      </a>
      . To avoid this,
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      Pa is extracted from the active zone of thorium
      <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_reactor" title="Molten salt reactor">
       molten salt reactors
      </a>
      during their operation, so that it only decays to
      <sup>
       233
      </sup>
      U.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_136-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-b1-136">
        [126]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another disadvantage of the thorium fuel cycle is the need to neutron-irradiate and process natural
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th before these advantages become real, and this requires more advanced technology than the presently used fuels based on uranium and plutonium; nevertheless, advances are being made in this technology.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder523_31-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder523-31">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another common criticism centres around the low commercial viability of the thorium fuel cycle:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        [127]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SovacoolValentine2012_138-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SovacoolValentine2012-138">
        [128]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
       <a href="#cite_note-139">
        [129]
       </a>
      </sup>
      some entities like the
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Energy_Agency" title="Nuclear Energy Agency">
       Nuclear Energy Agency
      </a>
      go further and predict that the thorium cycle will never be commercially viable while uranium is available in abundance—a situation which
      <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Findlay" title="Trevor Findlay">
       Trevor Findlay
      </a>
      predicts will persist "in the coming decades".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FindlayFindlay2011_140-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FindlayFindlay2011-140">
        [130]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Furthermore, though the isotopes produced in the thorium fuel cycle are not transuranic, some of them are still very dangerous, such as
      <sup>
       231
      </sup>
      Pa, which has a long half-life of 32760 years and is a major contributor to the long term
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiotoxic" title="Radiotoxic">
       radiotoxicity
      </a>
      of spent nuclear fuel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_136-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-b1-136">
        [126]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">
       Toxicity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Toxicity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Radiological">
       Radiological
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Radiological">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1237" data-file-width="1831" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png/220px-PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png/330px-PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png/440px-PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Experiment on the effect of radiation (from an unburned thorium gas mantle) on the germination and growth of
        <a href="/wiki/Timothy-grass" title="Timothy-grass">
         timothy-grass
        </a>
        seed; from
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Popular_Science" title="Popular Science">
          Popular Science
         </a>
        </i>
        , 1909.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Thorium is odourless and tasteless.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        [131]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As thorium occurs naturally, it exists in very small quantities almost everywhere on Earth: the average human contains about 100
      <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">
       micrograms
      </a>
      of thorium and typically consumes three micrograms per day of thorium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_142-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nbb-142">
        [132]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This exposure is raised for people who live near uranium, phosphate, or tin processing factories, thorium deposits, radioactive waste disposal sites, and for those who work in uranium, thorium, tin, or phosphate mining or gas mantle production industries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATSDR_143-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ATSDR-143">
        [133]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thorium is especially common in the
      <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">
       Tamil Nadu
      </a>
      coastal areas of India, where residents may be exposed to a naturally occurring radiation dose ten times higher than the worldwide average.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
       <a href="#cite_note-144">
        [134]
       </a>
      </sup>
      When thorium is ingested, 99.98% does not remain in the body. Out of the thorium that does remain in the body, three quarters of it accumulates in the
      <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">
       skeleton
      </a>
      . While absorption through the skin is possible, it is not a likely means of thorium exposure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_145-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-epa-145">
        [135]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Natural thorium decays very slowly compared to many other radioactive materials, and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_radiation" title="Alpha radiation">
       alpha radiation
      </a>
      emitted cannot penetrate human skin. As a result, owning and handling small amounts of thorium, such as those in a gas mantle, is considered safe, although usage of such items may pose some risks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_145-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-epa-145">
        [135]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Exposure to an aerosol of thorium, such as contaminated dust, can lead to increased risk of
      <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">
       cancers
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">
       lung
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">
       pancreas
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">
       blood
      </a>
      , as lungs and other internal organs can be penetrated by alpha radiation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_145-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-epa-145">
        [135]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Exposure to thorium internally leads to increased risk of
      <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">
       liver
      </a>
      diseases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arpansa_146-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-arpansa-146">
        [136]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Because
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th is only mildly radioactive, but decays to more dangerous radionuclides such as radium and radon, emitting alpha and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_radiation" title="Gamma radiation">
       gamma radiation
      </a>
      , a proper assessment of the radiological toxicity of
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th must include the contribution of its daughters such as
      <sup>
       228
      </sup>
      Ra, which are dangerous gamma emitters.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        [137]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are concerns about the safety of thorium mantles, as the dangerous daughters of thorium have much lower melting points than thoria (except of course
      <sup>
       228
      </sup>
      Th, which is chemically identical to its parent
      <sup>
       232
      </sup>
      Th), and they would be volatilised every time the mantle is heated for use. For example, during burning, significant fractions of the thorium daughters
      <sup>
       224
      </sup>
      Ra,
      <sup>
       228
      </sup>
      Ra,
      <sup>
       212
      </sup>
      Pb, and
      <sup>
       212
      </sup>
      Bi are released in the first hour of use alone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
       <a href="#cite_note-148">
        [138]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most of the radiation dose by a normal user arises from inhaling the radium, resulting in a radiation dose of up to 0.2
      <a href="/wiki/Sievert" title="Sievert">
       millisieverts
      </a>
      per use, about a third of the dose sustained during a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammogram" title="Mammogram">
       mammogram
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
       <a href="#cite_note-149">
        [139]
       </a>
      </sup>
      If a mantle is ingested, 0.4% of the thorium and 90% of its dangerous daughters are leached into the body.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poljanc_108-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Poljanc-108">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" title="Nuclear safety">
       nuclear safety
      </a>
      agencies make recommendations about their use, and have raised some safety concerns regarding their
      <a href="/wiki/Gas_mantle#Safety_concerns" title="Gas mantle">
       manufacture
      </a>
      and disposal, because while the radiation dose from one mantle is not a serious problem, that from many mantles gathered together in factories or landfills is.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arpansa_146-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-arpansa-146">
        [136]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">
       Chemical
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Chemical">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The chemical toxicity of thorium is low because thorium and its most common compounds (mostly the dioxide) are poorly soluble in water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneckenstein_150-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schneckenstein-150">
        [140]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nevertheless, some thorium compounds are chemically moderately
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">
       toxic
      </a>
      . People who work with thorium compounds are at a risk of
      <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis" title="Dermatitis">
       dermatitis
      </a>
      . It can take as much as thirty years after the ingestion of thorium for symptoms to manifest themselves.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_142-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nbb-142">
        [132]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Powdered thorium metal is pyrophoric and often ignites spontaneously in air.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder6163_6-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder6163-6">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the 1956
      <a href="/wiki/Sylvania_Electric_Products_explosion" title="Sylvania Electric Products explosion">
       Sylvania Electric Products explosion
      </a>
      , the burning of thorium metal powder
      <a href="/wiki/Sludge" title="Sludge">
       sludge
      </a>
      led to nine injuries, some severe, as well as one death from thorium poisoning.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP_151-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AP-151">
        [141]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
       <a href="#cite_note-152">
        [142]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newsday_153-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Newsday-153">
        [143]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
       Notes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Notes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
      <ol class="references">
       <li id="cite_note-3">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-3">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">
          Bismuth
         </a>
         is very slightly radioactive, but its half-life is so long that its decay is negligible even over geological timespans.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-26">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-26">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Gamma rays are distinguished by their origin in the nucleus, not their wavelength; hence there is no lower limit to gamma energy derived from radioactive decay.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
          <a href="#cite_note-25">
           [24]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-fissionable-30">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-fissionable_30-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-fissionable_30-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         A
         <i>
          fissionable
         </i>
         nuclide is capable of undergoing fission (even with a low probability) after capturing a high-energy neutron. Some of these nuclides can be induced to fission with low-energy thermal neutrons with a high probability; they are referred to as
         <i>
          fissile
         </i>
         . A
         <i>
          fertile
         </i>
         nuclide is one that could be bombarded with neutrons to produce a fissile nuclide.
         <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">
          Critical mass
         </a>
         is a mass of a ball of a material which could undergo a sustained
         <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">
          nuclear chain reaction
         </a>
         .
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-61">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-61">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         An even number of either protons or neutrons generally increases nuclear stability of isotopes, compared to isotopes with odd such numbers. For example, elements with odd atomic numbers have no more than two stable isotopes, while even-numbered elements have multiple stable isotopes, with tin (element 50) having the highest number of isotopes of all elements, ten.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_8-5">
          <a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-8">
           [7]
          </a>
         </sup>
         See
         <a href="/wiki/Even_and_odd_atomic_nuclei" title="Even and odd atomic nuclei">
          Even and odd atomic nuclei
         </a>
         for more details.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-66">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-66">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Other isotopes may occur alongside
         <sup>
          232
         </sup>
         Th, but only in trace quantities. If the source contains no uranium, the only other thorium isotope present would be
         <sup>
          228
         </sup>
         Th, which occurs in the
         <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">
          decay chain
         </a>
         of
         <sup>
          232
         </sup>
         Th (the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorium_series" title="Thorium series">
          thorium series
         </a>
         ): the ratio of
         <sup>
          228
         </sup>
         Th to
         <sup>
          232
         </sup>
         Th would be under 10
         <sup>
          −10
         </sup>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_15-7">
          <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-15">
           [14]
          </a>
         </sup>
         If uranium is present, tiny traces of several other isotopes will also be present:
         <sup>
          231
         </sup>
         Th and
         <sup>
          227
         </sup>
         Th from the decay chain of
         <sup>
          235
         </sup>
         U (the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actinium_series" title="Actinium series">
          actinium series
         </a>
         ), and slightly larger but still tiny traces of
         <sup>
          234
         </sup>
         Th and
         <sup>
          230
         </sup>
         Th from the decay chain of
         <sup>
          238
         </sup>
         U (the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium_series" title="Uranium series">
          uranium series
         </a>
         ).
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_15-8">
          <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-15">
           [14]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup>
          229
         </sup>
         Th is also been produced in the decay chain of
         <sup>
          237
         </sup>
         Np (the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neptunium_series" title="Neptunium series">
          neptunium series
         </a>
         ): while all primordial
         <sup>
          237
         </sup>
         Np is
         <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">
          extinct
         </a>
         , it is still produced today as a result of nuclear reactions in uranium ores.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
          <a href="#cite_note-65">
           [61]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup>
          229
         </sup>
         Th is mostly produced as a
         <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">
          daughter
         </a>
         of artificial
         <sup>
          233
         </sup>
         U, itself produced from
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_irradiation" title="Neutron irradiation">
          neutron irradiation
         </a>
         of
         <sup>
          232
         </sup>
         Th, due to its extreme rarity in nature.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder535_15-9">
          <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder535-15">
           [14]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-72">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-72">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Thorianite refers to minerals with 75–100 mol% ThO
         <sub>
          2
         </sub>
         ; uranothorianite, 25–75 mol% ThO
         <sub>
          2
         </sub>
         ; thorian uraninite, 15–25 mol% ThO
         <sub>
          2
         </sub>
         ;
         <a href="/wiki/Uraninite" title="Uraninite">
          uraninite
         </a>
         , 0–15 mol% ThO
         <sub>
          2
         </sub>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickleder556_67-7">
          <a href="#cite_note-Wickleder556-67">
           [62]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-87">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-87">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         At the time, the
         <a href="/wiki/Rare_earth_element" title="Rare earth element">
          rare earth elements
         </a>
         , among which thorium was found and with which it is closely associated in the nature, were thought to be divalent; this is shown by the fact that the rare earths are there given
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">
          atomic weight
         </a>
         values two-thirds of their actual ones, and thorium and uranium are given values half of their actual ones.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-91">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-91">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The main problem of isolating thorium lies not in its chemical electropositivity, but in the close association of thorium in nature with the rare earth elements and uranium, which collectively are difficult to separate from each other.
         <a href="/wiki/Lars_Fredrik_Nilson" title="Lars Fredrik Nilson">
          Lars Fredrik Nilson
         </a>
         , the discoverer of
         <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">
          scandium
         </a>
         , had previously made an attempt to isolate thorium metal in 1882, but was unsuccessful at achieving a high degree of purity.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
          <a href="#cite_note-90">
           [83]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-95">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-95">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Thorium also appears in
         <a href="/wiki/John_Newlands_(chemist)" title="John Newlands (chemist)">
          John Newlands
         </a>
         ' 1864 table as the last and heaviest element, as it was initially thought that uranium was a trivalent element with an atomic weight of around 120: this is half of its actual value, since uranium is predominantly hexavalent. It also appears as the heaviest element in
         <a href="/wiki/William_Odling" title="William Odling">
          William Odling
         </a>
         's 1864 table under titanium, zirconium, and
         <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">
          tantalum
         </a>
         . It does not appear in
         <a href="/wiki/Alexandre-%C3%89mile_B%C3%A9guyer_de_Chancourtois" title="Alexandre-Émile Béguyer de Chancourtois">
          Alexandre-Émile Béguyer de Chancourtois
         </a>
         ' 1862,
         <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Hinrichs" title="Gustav Hinrichs">
          Gustav Hinrichs
         </a>
         ' 1867, or
         <a href="/wiki/Julius_Lothar_Meyer" title="Julius Lothar Meyer">
          Julius Lothar Meyer
         </a>
         's 1870 periodic systems that exclude the rare earths and thorium.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leach_92-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-leach-92">
           [84]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-132">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-132">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The thirteen fissile actinide isotopes with half-lives over a year are
         <sup>
          229
         </sup>
         Th,
         <sup>
          233
         </sup>
         U,
         <sup>
          235
         </sup>
         U,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neptunium-236" title="Neptunium-236">
          <sup>
           236
          </sup>
          Np
         </a>
         ,
         <sup>
          239
         </sup>
         Pu,
         <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-241" title="Plutonium-241">
          <sup>
           241
          </sup>
          Pu
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Americium-242m" title="Americium-242m">
          <sup>
           242m
          </sup>
          Am
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curium-243" title="Curium-243">
          <sup>
           243
          </sup>
          Cm
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curium-245" title="Curium-245">
          <sup>
           245
          </sup>
          Cm
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curium-247" title="Curium-247">
          <sup>
           247
          </sup>
          Cm
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Californium-249" title="Californium-249">
          <sup>
           249
          </sup>
          Cf
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Californium-251" title="Californium-251">
          <sup>
           251
          </sup>
          Cf
         </a>
         , and
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einsteinium-252" title="Einsteinium-252">
          <sup>
           252
          </sup>
          Es
         </a>
         . Of these, only
         <sup>
          235
         </sup>
         U is naturally occurring, and only
         <sup>
          233
         </sup>
         U and
         <sup>
          239
         </sup>
         Pu can be bred from naturally occurring nuclei with single neutron capture.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ronen_130-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-Ronen-130">
           [121]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anucene_131-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-anucene-131">
           [122]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
      </ol>
     </div>

       <li id="cite_note-Intro2WMD-135">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-Intro2WMD_135-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Intro2WMD_135-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation book">
          Langford, R. Everett (2004).
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.amazon.com/dp/0471465607/" rel="nofollow">
           <i>
            Introduction to Weapons of Mass Destruction: Radiological, Chemical, and Biological
           </i>
          </a>
          .
          <a href="/wiki/Hoboken,_New_Jersey" title="Hoboken, New Jersey">
           Hoboken, New Jersey
          </a>
          :
          <a href="/wiki/John_Wiley_%26_Sons" title="John Wiley &amp; Sons">
           John Wiley &amp; Sons
          </a>
          . p. 85.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0471465607" title="Special:BookSources/0471465607">
           0471465607
          </a>
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            10 October
           </span>
           2012
          </span>
          .
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AThorium&amp;rft.aufirst=R.+Everett&amp;rft.aulast=Langford&amp;rft.btitle=Introduction+to+Weapons+of+Mass+Destruction%3A+Radiological%2C+Chemical%2C+and+Biological&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.amazon.com%2Fdp%2F0471465607%2F&amp;rft.isbn=0471465607&amp;rft.pages=85&amp;rft.place=Hoboken%2C+New+Jersey&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+%26+Sons&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
         <i>
          "The US tested a few uranium-233 bombs, but the presence of uranium-232 in the uranium-233 was a problem; the uranium-232 is a copious alpha emitter and tended to 'poison' the uranium-233 bomb by knocking stray neutrons from impurities in the bomb material, leading to possible pre-detonation. Separation of the uranium-232 from the uranium-233 proved to be very difficult and not practical. The uranium-233 bomb was never deployed since plutonium-239 was becoming plentiful."
         </i>
        </span>
       </li>
